<a href="/wiki/British_Aerospace_146" title="British Aerospace 146">BAe 146</a> CC2/C3</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The <b>Royal Air Force</b> (<b>RAF</b>) is the United Kingdom's <a href="/wiki/Air_force" title="Air force">aerial warfare force</a>. Formed towards the end of the <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">First World War</a> on 1 April 1918,<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[2]</a></sup> it is the oldest independent air force in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-Royal_Air_Force_90th_Anniversary_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Royal_Air_Force_90th_Anniversary-4">[3]</a></sup> Following victory over the <a href="/wiki/Central_Powers" title="Central Powers">Central Powers</a> in 1918 the RAF emerged as, at the time, the largest air force in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup> Since its formation, the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Royal_Air_Force" title="History of the Royal Air Force">RAF has taken a significant role</a> in <a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Military history of the United Kingdom">British military history</a>, in particular, playing a large part in the <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">Second World War</a> where it fought its most famous campaign, the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain" title="Battle of Britain">Battle of Britain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>The RAF's mission is to support the objectives of the British <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Defence_(United_Kingdom)" title="Ministry of Defence (United Kingdom)">Ministry of Defence</a> (MoD), which are to "provide the capabilities needed: to ensure the security and defence of the United Kingdom and overseas territories, including against terrorism; to support the Government’s foreign policy objectives particularly in promoting international peace and security".<sup id="cite_ref-RAF_.E2.80.93_Frequently_Asked_Questions_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RAF_.E2.80.93_Frequently_Asked_Questions-2">[1]</a></sup> The RAF describe its mission statement as "... [to provide] An <i>agile</i>, <i>adaptable</i> and <i>capable</i> Air Force that, person for person, is second to none, and that makes a decisive air power contribution in support of the UK Defence Mission".<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup> The mission statement is supported by the RAF's definition of <a href="/wiki/Air_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Air power">air power</a>, which guides its strategy. Air power is defined as "the ability to <a href="/wiki/Power_projection" title="Power projection">project power</a> from the air and space to influence the behaviour of people or the course of events".<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>Today the Royal Air Force maintains an operational fleet of <a href="/wiki/List_of_active_United_Kingdom_military_aircraft" title="List of active United Kingdom military aircraft">various types of aircraft</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup> described by the RAF as being "leading-edge" in terms of technology.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup> This largely consists of fixed-wing aircraft, including: <a href="/wiki/Fighter_aircraft" title="Fighter aircraft">fighter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Strike_aircraft" class="mw-redirect" title="Strike aircraft">strike</a> aircraft, <a href="/wiki/Airborne_early_warning_and_control" title="Airborne early warning and control">airborne early warning and control</a> aircraft, <a href="/wiki/ISTAR" class="mw-redirect" title="ISTAR">ISTAR</a> and <a href="/wiki/Signals_intelligence" title="Signals intelligence">SIGINT</a> aircraft, <a href="/wiki/Aerial_refueling" title="Aerial refueling">aerial refueling</a> aircraft and <a href="/wiki/Strategic_airlift" class="mw-redirect" title="Strategic airlift">strategic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tactical_airlift" class="mw-redirect" title="Tactical airlift">tactical transport</a> aircraft. The majority of the RAF's rotary-wing aircraft form part of the tri-service <a href="/wiki/Joint_Helicopter_Command" title="Joint Helicopter Command">Joint Helicopter Command</a> in support of ground forces. Most of the RAF's aircraft and personnel are based in the UK, with many others serving on operations (principally <a href="/wiki/Operation_Shader" title="Operation Shader">over Iraq and Syria</a>) or at long-established overseas bases (<a href="/wiki/RAF_Ascension_Island" title="RAF Ascension Island">Ascension Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Forces_Cyprus" title="British Forces Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Gibraltar" title="RAF Gibraltar">Gibraltar</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/RAF_Mount_Pleasant" title="RAF Mount Pleasant">Falkland Islands</a>). Although the RAF is the principal British air power arm, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Air_Arm" title="Fleet Air Arm">Fleet Air Arm</a> and the <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Army_Air_Corps_(United_Kingdom)" title="Army Air Corps (United Kingdom)">Army Air Corps</a> also deliver air power which is integrated into the maritime, littoral and land environments.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Air_Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Royal_Air_Force" title="History of the Royal Air Force">History of the Royal Air Force</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Royal_Air_Force_operations" title="List of Royal Air Force operations">List of Royal Air Force operations</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Air_Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While the British were not the first to make use of heavier-than-air military aircraft, the RAF is the world's oldest independent air force: that is, the first air force to become independent of army or navy control.<sup id="cite_ref-Royal_Air_Force_90th_Anniversary_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Royal_Air_Force_90th_Anniversary-4">[3]</a></sup> It was founded on 1 April 1918, with headquarters located in the former <a href="/wiki/Hotel_Cecil_(London)" title="Hotel Cecil (London)">Hotel Cecil</a>, during the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a>, by the amalgamation of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Flying_Corps" title="Royal Flying Corps">Royal Flying Corps</a> (RFC) and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Naval_Air_Service" title="Royal Naval Air Service">Royal Naval Air Service</a> (RNAS). At that time it was the largest air force in the world. After the war, the service was drastically cut and its inter-war years were relatively quiet, with the RAF taking responsibility for <a href="/wiki/RAF_Iraq_Command" title="RAF Iraq Command">the control of Iraq</a> and executing a number of minor actions in other parts of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>. The RAF's naval aviation branch, the <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Air_Arm" title="Fleet Air Arm">Fleet Air Arm</a>, was founded in 1924 but handed over to <a href="/wiki/Admiralty" title="Admiralty">Admiralty</a> control on 24 May 1939.</p>
<p>The RAF developed the doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Strategic_bombing" title="Strategic bombing">strategic bombing</a> which led to the construction of long-range bombers and became its main bombing strategy in the Second World War.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_World_War">Second World War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Air_Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Second World War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ray_Flying_Legends_2005-1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Ray_Flying_Legends_2005-1.jpg/220px-Ray_Flying_Legends_2005-1.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Ray_Flying_Legends_2005-1.jpg/330px-Ray_Flying_Legends_2005-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Ray_Flying_Legends_2005-1.jpg/440px-Ray_Flying_Legends_2005-1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ray_Flying_Legends_2005-1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A later version of the <a href="/wiki/Supermarine_Spitfire" title="Supermarine Spitfire">Spitfires</a> which played a major part in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain" title="Battle of Britain">Battle of Britain</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Air_warfare_of_World_War_II" title="Air warfare of World War II">Air warfare of World War II</a></div>
<p>The RAF underwent rapid expansion prior to and during the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>. Under the <a href="/wiki/British_Commonwealth_Air_Training_Plan" title="British Commonwealth Air Training Plan">British Commonwealth Air Training Plan</a> of December 1939, the air forces of <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">British Commonwealth</a> countries trained and formed "<a href="/wiki/Article_XV_squadrons" title="Article XV squadrons">Article XV squadrons</a>" for service with RAF formations. Many individual personnel from these countries, and exiles from <a href="/wiki/Occupied_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Occupied Europe">occupied Europe</a>, also served with RAF squadrons. By the end of the war the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Canadian_Air_Force" title="Royal Canadian Air Force">Royal Canadian Air Force</a> had contributed more than 30 squadrons to serve in RAF formations, similarly, approximately a quarter of Bomber Command's personnel were Canadian.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> Additionally, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Australian_Air_Force" title="Royal Australian Air Force">Royal Australian Air Force</a> represented around nine percent of all RAF personnel who served in the European and Mediterranean theatres.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Avro_683_Lancaster_B1,_UK_-_Air_Force_AN1207411.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Avro_683_Lancaster_B1%2C_UK_-_Air_Force_AN1207411.jpg/220px-Avro_683_Lancaster_B1%2C_UK_-_Air_Force_AN1207411.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Avro_683_Lancaster_B1%2C_UK_-_Air_Force_AN1207411.jpg/330px-Avro_683_Lancaster_B1%2C_UK_-_Air_Force_AN1207411.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Avro_683_Lancaster_B1%2C_UK_-_Air_Force_AN1207411.jpg/440px-Avro_683_Lancaster_B1%2C_UK_-_Air_Force_AN1207411.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="678" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Avro_683_Lancaster_B1,_UK_-_Air_Force_AN1207411.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Avro_Lancaster_PA474" title="Avro Lancaster PA474">Avro Lancaster</a> heavy bomber was extensively used during the strategic bombing of Germany.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain" title="Battle of Britain">Battle of Britain</a> in 1940, the RAF (supplemented by 2 Fleet Air Arm Squadrons, Polish, Czecho-Slovak and other multinational pilots and ground personnel) defended the skies over Britain against the numerically superior German <a href="/wiki/Luftwaffe" title="Luftwaffe">Luftwaffe</a>. In what is perhaps the most prolonged and complicated air campaign in history, the Battle of Britain contributed significantly to the delay and subsequent indefinite postponement of <a href="/wiki/Hitler" class="mw-redirect" title="Hitler">Hitler</a>'s plans for an invasion of the United Kingdom (<a href="/wiki/Operation_Sealion" class="mw-redirect" title="Operation Sealion">Operation Sealion</a>). In the House of Commons on 20 August, prompted by the ongoing efforts of the RAF, Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> eloquently made a speech to the nation, where he said "<a href="/wiki/Never_was_so_much_owed_by_so_many_to_so_few" title="Never was so much owed by so many to so few">Never in the field of human conflict was so much owed by so many to so few"</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>The largest RAF effort during the war was the <a href="/wiki/Strategic_bombing" title="Strategic bombing">strategic bombing</a> campaign against Germany by <a href="/wiki/RAF_Bomber_Command" title="RAF Bomber Command">Bomber Command</a>. While RAF bombing of Germany began almost immediately upon the outbreak of war, under the leadership of Air Chief Marshal <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Travers_Harris" class="mw-redirect" title="Arthur Travers Harris">Harris</a>, these attacks became increasingly devastating from 1942 onward as new technology and greater numbers of superior aircraft became available. The RAF adopted night-time <a href="/wiki/Area_bombing" class="mw-redirect" title="Area bombing">area bombing</a> on German cities such as <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Hamburg_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Hamburg in World War II">Hamburg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Dresden" class="mw-redirect" title="Bombing of Dresden">Dresden</a>, and developed precision bombing techniques for specific operations, such as the <a href="/wiki/Operation_Chastise" title="Operation Chastise">"Dambusters" raid</a> by <a href="/wiki/No._617_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 617 Squadron RAF">No. 617 Squadron</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> or the Amiens prison raid known as <a href="/wiki/Operation_Jericho" title="Operation Jericho">Operation Jericho</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cold_War_era">Cold War era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Air_Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Cold War era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Royal_Air_Force&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(July 2017)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a></div>
<p>Following victory in the Second World War, the RAF underwent significant re-organisation, as technological advances in air warfare saw the arrival of jet fighters and bombers. During the early stages of the Cold War, one of the first major operations undertaken by the Royal Air Force was in 1948 and the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Airlift" class="mw-redirect" title="Berlin Airlift">Berlin Airlift</a>, codenamed Operation Plainfire. Between 26 June and the lifting of the Russian blockade of the city on 2 May, the RAF provided 17% of the total supplies delivered during the event, using Avro Yorks, Douglas Dakotas flying to <a href="/wiki/Gatow_Airport" class="mw-redirect" title="Gatow Airport">Gatow Airport</a> and Short Sunderlands flying to Lake Havel.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Raf_victor_in_1961_arp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Raf_victor_in_1961_arp.jpg/220px-Raf_victor_in_1961_arp.jpg" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Raf_victor_in_1961_arp.jpg/330px-Raf_victor_in_1961_arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Raf_victor_in_1961_arp.jpg/440px-Raf_victor_in_1961_arp.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1918" data-file-height="1297" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Raf_victor_in_1961_arp.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Handley_Page_Victor" title="Handley Page Victor">Handley Page Victor</a> bomber was a <a href="/wiki/Strategic_bomber" title="Strategic bomber">strategic bomber</a> of the RAF's <a href="/wiki/V_bomber" title="V bomber">V bomber force</a> used to carry both conventional and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_bombs" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear bombs">nuclear bombs</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Before Britain developed its own <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapons</a> the RAF was provided with American nuclear weapons under <a href="/wiki/Project_E" title="Project E">Project E</a>. However following the development of its own arsenal, the British Government elected on 16 February 1960 to share the country's <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom" title="Nuclear weapons and the United Kingdom">nuclear deterrent</a> between the RAF and submarines of the Royal Navy, first deciding on 13 April to concentrate solely on the air force's <a href="/wiki/V_bomber" title="V bomber">V bomber</a> fleet. These were initially armed with nuclear <a href="/wiki/Gravity_bomb" class="mw-redirect" title="Gravity bomb">gravity bombs</a>, later being equipped with the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Steel_missile" class="mw-redirect" title="Blue Steel missile">Blue Steel missile</a>. Following the development of the <a href="/wiki/Polaris_missile" class="mw-redirect" title="Polaris missile">Royal Navy's Polaris submarines</a>, the strategic nuclear deterrent passed to the navy's submarines on 30 June 1969.<sup id="cite_ref-RAF_1960-69_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RAF_1960-69-17">[16]</a></sup> With the introduction of Polaris, the RAF's strategic nuclear role was reduced to a tactical one, using <a href="/wiki/WE.177" title="WE.177">WE.177</a> gravity bombs. This tactical role was continued by the V bombers into the 1980s and until 1998 by <a href="/wiki/RAF_Tornado_GR1" class="mw-redirect" title="RAF Tornado GR1">Tornado GR1s</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Avro_Vulcan_Bomber_RAF.JPEG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Avro_Vulcan_Bomber_RAF.JPEG/220px-Avro_Vulcan_Bomber_RAF.JPEG" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Avro_Vulcan_Bomber_RAF.JPEG/330px-Avro_Vulcan_Bomber_RAF.JPEG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Avro_Vulcan_Bomber_RAF.JPEG/440px-Avro_Vulcan_Bomber_RAF.JPEG 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1979" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Avro_Vulcan_Bomber_RAF.JPEG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Avro_Vulcan" title="Avro Vulcan">Avro Vulcan</a> was a <a href="/wiki/Strategic_bomber" title="Strategic bomber">strategic bomber</a> used during the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> to carry conventional and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom" title="Nuclear weapons and the United Kingdom">nuclear bombs</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>For much of the Cold War the primary role of the RAF was the defence of <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> against potential attack by the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, with many <a href="/wiki/RAF_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="RAF Germany">squadrons based in West Germany</a>. With the decline of the British Empire, global operations were scaled back, and <a href="/wiki/RAF_Far_East_Air_Force" class="mw-redirect" title="RAF Far East Air Force">RAF Far East Air Force</a> was disbanded on 31 October 1971. Despite this, the RAF fought in many battles in the Cold War period. In June 1948 the RAF commenced Operation Firedog against Malayan terrorists during the <a href="/wiki/Malayan_Emergency" title="Malayan Emergency">Malayan Emergency</a>. Operations continued for the next 12 years until 1960 with aircraft flying out of <a href="/wiki/RAF_Tengah" class="mw-redirect" title="RAF Tengah">RAF Tengah</a> and <a href="/wiki/RAF_Butterworth" class="mw-redirect" title="RAF Butterworth">RAF Butterworth</a>. The RAF played a minor role in the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Flying_boat" title="Flying boat">flying boats</a> taking part. From 1953 to 1956 the RAF Avro Lincoln squadrons carried out anti-<a href="/wiki/Mau_Mau" class="mw-redirect" title="Mau Mau">Mau Mau</a> operations in <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> using its base at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Eastleigh" class="mw-redirect" title="RAF Eastleigh">RAF Eastleigh</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Suez_Crisis" title="Suez Crisis">Suez Crisis</a> in 1956 saw a large RAF role, with aircraft operating from <a href="/wiki/RAF_Akrotiri" title="RAF Akrotiri">RAF Akrotiri</a> and <a href="/wiki/RAF_Nicosia" title="RAF Nicosia">RAF Nicosia</a> on <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> and <a href="/wiki/RAF_Luqa" title="RAF Luqa">RAF Luqa</a> and <a href="/wiki/RAF_Hal_Far" title="RAF Hal Far">RAF Hal Far</a> on <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a> as part of <a href="/wiki/Operation_Musketeer_(1956)" title="Operation Musketeer (1956)">Operation Musketeer</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Konfrontasi" class="mw-redirect" title="Konfrontasi">Konfrontasi</a> against Indonesia in the early 1960s did see use of RAF aircraft, but due to a combination of deft diplomacy and selective ignoring of certain events by both sides, it never developed into a full-scale war.</p>
<p>One of the largest actions undertaken by the RAF during the cold war was the air campaign during the 1982 <a href="/wiki/Falklands_War" title="Falklands War">Falklands War</a>, in which the RAF operated alongside the <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Air_Arm" title="Fleet Air Arm">Fleet Air Arm</a>. During the war, RAF aircraft were deployed in the mid-Atlantic at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Ascension_Island" title="RAF Ascension Island">RAF Ascension Island</a> and a detachment from No. 1 Squadron was deployed with the Royal Navy, operating from the aircraft carrier <a href="/wiki/HMS_Hermes_(R12)" title="HMS Hermes (R12)">HMS <i>Hermes</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ashworth_p26_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ashworth_p26-18">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Evans_p74-5_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans_p74-5-19">[18]</a></sup> RAF pilots also flew missions using the Royal Navy's <a href="/wiki/Sea_Harrier" class="mw-redirect" title="Sea Harrier">Sea Harriers</a> in the air-to-air combat role.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup> Following a British victory, the RAF remained in the <a href="/wiki/South_Atlantic" class="mw-redirect" title="South Atlantic">South Atlantic</a> to provide air defence to the Falkland Islands, based at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Mount_Pleasant" title="RAF Mount Pleasant">RAF Mount Pleasant</a> (built 1984).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_history">Recent history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Air_Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Recent history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>With the end of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> and the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, the RAF's focus has returned to delivering expeditionary air power.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> Since 1990 the RAF has been involved in several large-scale operations, including: the 1991 <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf War</a>, the 1999 <a href="/wiki/Kosovo_War" title="Kosovo War">Kosovo War</a>, the 2001 <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001–present)">War in Afghanistan</a>, the <a href="/wiki/2003_invasion_of_Iraq" title="2003 invasion of Iraq">2003 invasion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">war in Iraq</a> and the 2011 <a href="/wiki/2011_military_intervention_in_Libya" title="2011 military intervention in Libya">intervention in Libya</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Red_Arrows_and_Typhoons_flypast_for_90th_Anniversary_of_the_RAF_MOD_45147908.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Red_Arrows_and_Typhoons_flypast_for_90th_Anniversary_of_the_RAF_MOD_45147908.jpg/220px-Red_Arrows_and_Typhoons_flypast_for_90th_Anniversary_of_the_RAF_MOD_45147908.jpg" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Red_Arrows_and_Typhoons_flypast_for_90th_Anniversary_of_the_RAF_MOD_45147908.jpg/330px-Red_Arrows_and_Typhoons_flypast_for_90th_Anniversary_of_the_RAF_MOD_45147908.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Red_Arrows_and_Typhoons_flypast_for_90th_Anniversary_of_the_RAF_MOD_45147908.jpg/440px-Red_Arrows_and_Typhoons_flypast_for_90th_Anniversary_of_the_RAF_MOD_45147908.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3306" data-file-height="2298" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Red_Arrows_and_Typhoons_flypast_for_90th_Anniversary_of_the_RAF_MOD_45147908.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Eurofighter_Typhoon" title="Eurofighter Typhoon">Typhoons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Red_Arrows" title="Red Arrows">Red Arrows</a> flypast for the 90th Anniversary of the RAF, 2008</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The RAF's 90th anniversary was commemorated on 1 April 2008 by a flypast of 9 <a href="/wiki/Red_Arrows" title="Red Arrows">Red Arrows</a> and four <a href="/wiki/Eurofighter_Typhoon" title="Eurofighter Typhoon">Typhoons</a> along the <a href="/wiki/Thames" class="mw-redirect" title="Thames">Thames</a>, in a straight line from just south of <a href="/wiki/London_City_Airport" title="London City Airport">London City Airport</a> Tower Bridge, the <a href="/wiki/London_Eye" title="London Eye">London Eye</a>, the <a href="/wiki/RAF_Memorial" class="mw-redirect" title="RAF Memorial">RAF Memorial</a> and (at 13.00) the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Defence_(United_Kingdom)" title="Ministry of Defence (United Kingdom)">Ministry of Defence</a> building.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Four major defence reviews have been conducted since the end of the Cold War: the 1990 <a href="/wiki/Options_for_Change" title="Options for Change">Options for Change</a>, the 1998 <a href="/wiki/Strategic_Defence_Review" title="Strategic Defence Review">Strategic Defence Review</a>, the 2003 <a href="/wiki/Delivering_Security_in_a_Changing_World" title="Delivering Security in a Changing World">Delivering Security in a Changing World</a> and the 2010 <a href="/wiki/Strategic_Defence_and_Security_Review_2010" title="Strategic Defence and Security Review 2010">Strategic Defence and Security Review</a>. All four defence reviews have resulted in steady reductions in manpower and numbers of aircraft, especially combat aircraft such as fast-jets. As part of the latest 2010 Strategic Defence and Security Review, the <a href="/wiki/BAE_Systems_Nimrod_MRA4" title="BAE Systems Nimrod MRA4">BAE Systems Nimrod MRA4</a> maritime patrol aircraft was cancelled due to over spending and missing deadlines.<sup id="cite_ref-defensenews_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-defensenews-25">[24]</a></sup> Other reductions saw total RAF manpower reduced by 5,000 personnel to a trained strength of 33,000 and the early retirement of the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Force_Harrier" title="Joint Force Harrier">Joint Force Harrier</a> aircraft, the <a href="/wiki/BAE_Harrier_II" class="mw-redirect" title="BAE Harrier II">Harrier GR7/GR9</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RAF_QRA_Typhoon_Intercepting_Russian_Bear_Aircraft_MOD_45158137.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/RAF_QRA_Typhoon_Intercepting_Russian_Bear_Aircraft_MOD_45158137.jpg/220px-RAF_QRA_Typhoon_Intercepting_Russian_Bear_Aircraft_MOD_45158137.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/RAF_QRA_Typhoon_Intercepting_Russian_Bear_Aircraft_MOD_45158137.jpg/330px-RAF_QRA_Typhoon_Intercepting_Russian_Bear_Aircraft_MOD_45158137.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/RAF_QRA_Typhoon_Intercepting_Russian_Bear_Aircraft_MOD_45158137.jpg/440px-RAF_QRA_Typhoon_Intercepting_Russian_Bear_Aircraft_MOD_45158137.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1070" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RAF_QRA_Typhoon_Intercepting_Russian_Bear_Aircraft_MOD_45158137.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Eurofighter_Typhoon" title="Eurofighter Typhoon">Typhoon</a> on QRA intercepts a Russian <a href="/wiki/Tupolev_Tu-95" title="Tupolev Tu-95">Tupolev Tu-95</a> bomber approaching UK airspace.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In recent years fighter aircraft on Quick Reaction Alert (QRA) have been increasingly required to <a href="/wiki/Scrambling_(military)" title="Scrambling (military)">scramble</a> in response to efforts made by the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Air_Force" title="Russian Air Force">Russian Air Force</a> to approach British airspace.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> On 24 January 2014 in the Houses of Parliament, <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a> MP and <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_State_for_the_Armed_Forces" title="Minister of State for the Armed Forces">Minister of State for the Armed Forces</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Robathan" title="Andrew Robathan">Andrew Robathan</a>, announced that the RAF's QRA force had been scrambled almost thirty times in the last three years: eleven times during 2010, ten times during 2011 and eight times during 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>RAF Coningsby in Lincolnshire and RAF Lossiemouth in Moray both provide Quick Reaction Alert, or QRA, and scramble their fighter jets within minutes to meet or intercept aircraft which give cause for concern. Lossiemouth generally covers the northern sector, while Coningsby provides QRA in the south. Typhoon pilot Flight Lieutenant Noel Rees describes how QRA duty works. "At the start of the scaled QRA response, civilian air traffic controllers might see on their screens an aircraft behaving erratically, not responding to their radio calls, or note that it’s transmitting a distress signal through its transponder. Rather than scramble Typhoons at the first hint of something abnormal, a controller has the option to put them on a higher level of alert, ‘a call to cockpit’. In this scenario the pilot races to the hardened aircraft shelter and does everything short of starting his engines".<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 4 October 2015, a final stand-down saw the end of more than 70 years of RAF Search and Rescue provision in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-Farewell_to_RAF_UK_Search_.26_Rescue_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Farewell_to_RAF_UK_Search_.26_Rescue-30">[29]</a></sup> The RAF and Royal Navy's <a href="/wiki/Westland_Sea_King" title="Westland Sea King">Westland Sea King</a> fleets, after over 30 years of service, were retired. A civilian contractor, <a href="/wiki/Bristow_Helicopters" title="Bristow Helicopters">Bristow Helicopters</a>, has taken over responsibility for UK Search and Rescue, under a <a href="/wiki/Private_Finance_Initiative" class="mw-redirect" title="Private Finance Initiative">Private Finance Initiative</a> with newly purchased <a href="/wiki/Sikorsky_S-92" title="Sikorsky S-92">Sikorsky S-92</a> and <a href="/wiki/AgustaWestland_AW189" title="AgustaWestland AW189">AgustaWestland AW189</a> aircraft. While the Royal Navy's SAR force will continue to operate until the end of the year, the new contract means that by 2016, all UK SAR coverage will be provided by Bristow's.</p>

<p>The professional head of the RAF is the <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_the_Air_Staff_(United_Kingdom)" title="Chief of the Air Staff (United Kingdom)">Chief of the Air Staff</a> (CAS). The CAS heads the <a href="/wiki/Air_Force_Board" title="Air Force Board">Air Force Board</a>, which is a committee of the <a href="/wiki/Defence_Council_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Defence Council of the United Kingdom">Defence Council</a>. The Air Force Board is the management board of the RAF and consists of several high-ranking officers.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Command">Command</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Air_Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Command">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Royal_Air_Force_commands" title="List of Royal Air Force commands">List of Royal Air Force commands</a></div>
<p>Authority is delegated from the Air Force Board to the RAF's <a href="/wiki/Command_(military_formation)" title="Command (military formation)">command</a>. While there were once individual commands responsible for bombers, fighters, training, etc., now only the <a href="/wiki/RAF_Air_Command" title="RAF Air Command">Air Command</a> exists, headquartered at <a href="/wiki/RAF_High_Wycombe" title="RAF High Wycombe">RAF High Wycombe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Groups">Groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Air_Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Royal_Air_Force_groups" title="List of Royal Air Force groups">List of Royal Air Force groups</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Group_(air_force)" class="mw-redirect" title="Group (air force)">Groups</a> are the subdivisions of operational commands; these are responsible for certain types of operation or for operations in limited geographical areas. As from July 2014, four groups exist. Additionally there is an expeditionary air group.</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/No._1_Group_RAF" title="No. 1 Group RAF">No. 1 Group</a> (Air Combat): controls the RAF's <a href="/wiki/Intelligence,_Surveillance,_Target_Acquisition,_and_Reconnaissance" class="mw-redirect" title="Intelligence, Surveillance, Target Acquisition, and Reconnaissance">Intelligence, Surveillance, Target Acquisition, and Reconnaissance</a> (ISTAR), and combat fast air aircraft and Air Battlespace Management assets as well as the following <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force_station" title="Royal Air Force station">stations</a>: <a href="/wiki/RAF_Leeming" title="RAF Leeming">RAF Leeming</a>, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Coningsby" title="RAF Coningsby">RAF Coningsby</a>, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Marham" title="RAF Marham">RAF Marham</a>, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Waddington" title="RAF Waddington">RAF Waddington</a>, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Boulmer" title="RAF Boulmer">RAF Boulmer</a>, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Scampton" title="RAF Scampton">RAF Scampton</a> and <a href="/wiki/RAF_Lossiemouth" title="RAF Lossiemouth">RAF Lossiemouth</a> in the UK. The RAF's <a href="/wiki/Electronic_warfare" title="Electronic warfare">electronic warfare</a> tactics range at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Spadeadam" title="RAF Spadeadam">RAF Spadeadam</a> in Cumbria, is also within its sphere of responsibility.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/No._2_Group_RAF" title="No. 2 Group RAF">No. 2 Group</a> (Air Combat Support): controls the Air Mobility aircraft and the RAF's Force Protection assets as well as the following station: <a href="/wiki/RAF_Brize_Norton" title="RAF Brize Norton">RAF Brize Norton</a>, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Honington" title="RAF Honington">RAF Honington</a> and <a href="/wiki/RAF_Northolt" title="RAF Northolt">RAF Northolt</a>. The Group also provides single-Service oversight of the RAF elements on the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Helicopter_Command" title="Joint Helicopter Command">Joint Helicopter Command</a> including <a href="/wiki/RAF_Benson" title="RAF Benson">RAF Benson</a>, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Odiham" title="RAF Odiham">RAF Odiham</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tactical_Supply_Wing_RAF" title="Tactical Supply Wing RAF">Tactical Supply Wing</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/No._22_Group_RAF" title="No. 22 Group RAF">No. 22 (Training) Group</a>: responsible for training, including flying and technical training for all 3 Services, as well as the following stations: <a href="/wiki/RAF_Cranwell" title="RAF Cranwell">RAF College Cranwell</a>, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Cosford" title="RAF Cosford">RAF Cosford</a>, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Halton" title="RAF Halton">RAF Halton</a>, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Linton-on-Ouse" title="RAF Linton-on-Ouse">RAF Linton-on-Ouse</a>, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Shawbury" title="RAF Shawbury">RAF Shawbury</a>, and <a href="/wiki/RAF_Valley" title="RAF Valley">RAF Valley</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/No._38_Group_RAF" title="No. 38 Group RAF">No. 38 Group</a> (Air Combat Service Support): engineering, logistics, communications, medical operations and musical units as well as <a href="/wiki/RAF_High_Wycombe" title="RAF High Wycombe">RAF High Wycombe</a> and <a href="/wiki/RAF_Wittering" title="RAF Wittering">RAF Wittering</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/No._83_Expeditionary_Air_Group_(United_Kingdom)" title="No. 83 Expeditionary Air Group (United Kingdom)">No. 83 Expeditionary Air Group</a>, under the command of the <a href="/wiki/Permanent_Joint_Headquarters" title="Permanent Joint Headquarters">Permanent Joint Headquarters</a>, is active in the Middle East, currently supporting the UK's military intervention against <a href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant" title="Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant">ISIL</a> (<a href="/wiki/Operation_Shader" title="Operation Shader">Operation Shader</a>). The expeditionary air group also supported operations over Afghanistan (<a href="/wiki/Operation_Herrick" title="Operation Herrick">Operation Herrick</a>) and Iraq (<a href="/wiki/Operation_Telic" title="Operation Telic">Operation Telic</a>).</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stations">Stations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Air_Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Stations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force_station" title="Royal Air Force station">Royal Air Force station</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Radar_RAF_Fylingdales.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Radar_RAF_Fylingdales.jpg/220px-Radar_RAF_Fylingdales.jpg" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Radar_RAF_Fylingdales.jpg/330px-Radar_RAF_Fylingdales.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Radar_RAF_Fylingdales.jpg/440px-Radar_RAF_Fylingdales.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3211" data-file-height="2200" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Radar_RAF_Fylingdales.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Phased_Array" class="mw-redirect" title="Phased Array">Phased Array</a> <a href="/wiki/Ballistic_Missile_Early_Warning_System" title="Ballistic Missile Early Warning System">Ballistic Missile Early Warning System</a> at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Fylingdales" title="RAF Fylingdales">RAF Fylingdales</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>An <a href="/wiki/RAF_station" class="mw-redirect" title="RAF station">RAF station</a> is ordinarily subordinate to a group and it is administratively sub-divided into wings. Since the mid to late 1930s RAF stations have controlled a number of flying squadrons or other units at one location by means of a station headquarters.</p>
<p>As the Air Force has reduced in size and modernised, the squadrons and aircraft types have tended towards groupings at fewer, larger bases. Examples include the ISTAR fleet at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Waddington" title="RAF Waddington">RAF Waddington</a> and the Support Helicopter fleet of Puma and Chinook aircraft split between <a href="/wiki/RAF_Odiham" title="RAF Odiham">RAF Odiham</a> and <a href="/wiki/RAF_Benson" title="RAF Benson">RAF Benson</a>. Nearly the entire transport fleet now resides at the hub of <a href="/wiki/RAF_Brize_Norton" title="RAF Brize Norton">RAF Brize Norton</a>, following the closure of <a href="/wiki/RAF_Lyneham" class="mw-redirect" title="RAF Lyneham">RAF Lyneham</a>. The continuous reduction in aircraft numbers since the end of the Cold War made it uneconomical to support operations at multiple bases.</p>
<p>The RAF still operates a number of overseas bases to support global operations. <a href="/wiki/RAF_Akrotiri" title="RAF Akrotiri">RAF Akrotiri</a> is a key staging post for operations in the Middle East, and <a href="/wiki/RAF_Mount_Pleasant" title="RAF Mount Pleasant">RAF Mount Pleasant</a> enables a vital air bridge between the United Kingdom and the <a href="/wiki/Falkland_Islands" title="Falkland Islands">Falkland Islands</a>, as well as providing a base for Typhoon air defence aircraft.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wings">Wings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Air_Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Wings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Royal_Air_Force_wings" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Royal Air Force wings">List of Royal Air Force wings</a></div>

<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:United_Kingdom_relief_location_map.jpg" title="File:United Kingdom relief location map.jpg"></a></div>
<b>Primary RAF Flying Stations</b> Fast Jet: Red, ISTAR: Blue, Transport: Orange, Support Helicopter: Green Training: Yellow</div>

</div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Wing_(air_force_unit)" class="mw-redirect" title="Wing (air force unit)">wing</a> is either an operational sub-division of a group or an administrative sub-division of an RAF station.</p>
<p>Independent Wings are a grouping of two or more squadrons, either flying squadrons or ground support squadrons. In former times, numbered flying wings have existed, but recently they have been created only when required. For example, during <a href="/wiki/Operation_Telic" title="Operation Telic">Operation Telic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Panavia_Tornado" title="Panavia Tornado">Tornado GR4</a> wings were formed to operate from <a href="/wiki/Ali_Al_Salem_Air_Base" title="Ali Al Salem Air Base">Ali Al Salem</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al_Udeid_Air_Base" title="Al Udeid Air Base">Al Udeid</a> air bases and the <a href="/wiki/Panavia_Tornado_ADV" title="Panavia Tornado ADV">Tornado F3</a> equipped <a href="/wiki/RAF_Leuchars" class="mw-redirect" title="RAF Leuchars">Leuchars</a> Fighter Wing at <a href="/wiki/PSAB" class="mw-redirect" title="PSAB">Prince Sultan Air Base</a>; each of these were made up of aircraft and crews from several squadrons.</p>
<p>On 31 March 2006, the RAF formed nine <a href="/wiki/Expeditionary_Air_Wing" title="Expeditionary Air Wing">Expeditionary Air Wings</a> (EAWs) to support operations. They were established at the nine main operating bases; <a href="/wiki/RAF_Coningsby" title="RAF Coningsby">RAF Coningsby</a>, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Cottesmore" title="RAF Cottesmore">RAF Cottesmore</a>, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Kinloss" title="RAF Kinloss">RAF Kinloss</a>, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Leeming" title="RAF Leeming">RAF Leeming</a>, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Leuchars" class="mw-redirect" title="RAF Leuchars">RAF Leuchars</a>, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Lossiemouth" title="RAF Lossiemouth">RAF Lossiemouth</a>, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Lyneham" class="mw-redirect" title="RAF Lyneham">RAF Lyneham</a>, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Marham" title="RAF Marham">RAF Marham</a> and <a href="/wiki/RAF_Waddington" title="RAF Waddington">RAF Waddington</a> numbered Nos 121, 122, 325, 135, 125, 140, 38, 138 and 34 EAWs respectively. These units are commanded by a <a href="/wiki/Group_Captain" class="mw-redirect" title="Group Captain">Group Captain</a> who is also the parent unit's Station Commander. The EAW comprises the non-formed unit elements of the station that are required to support a deployed operating base, i.e., the command and control, logistics and administration functions amongst others. They are designed to be flexible and quickly adaptable for differing operations. They are independent of flying squadrons, Air Combat Support Units (ACSU) and Air Combat Service Support Units (ACSSU) who are attached to the EAW depending on the task it has been assigned.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>A wing is also an administrative sub-division of an RAF station. Historically, for a flying station these were normally Operations Wing, Engineering Wing and Administration Wing and each wing was commanded by an officer of <a href="/wiki/Wing_commander_(rank)" title="Wing commander (rank)">wing commander</a> rank. Early in the 21st century, the model changed, with Engineering Wing typically being split into Forward Support Wing and Depth Support Wing, while Administration Wing was redesignated Base Support Wing.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Squadrons">Squadrons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Air_Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Squadrons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Royal_Air_Force_aircraft_squadrons" title="List of Royal Air Force aircraft squadrons">List of Royal Air Force aircraft squadrons</a></div>
<p>A flying squadron is an aircraft unit which carries out the primary tasks of the RAF. RAF squadrons are somewhat analogous to the regiments of the British Army in that they have histories and traditions going back to their formation, regardless of where they are based, which aircraft they are operating, etc. They can be awarded <a href="/wiki/Flag" title="Flag">standards</a> and <a href="/wiki/RAF_battle_honours" title="RAF battle honours">battle honours</a> for meritorious service. Whilst every squadron is different, most flying squadrons are commanded by a <a href="/wiki/Wing_Commander_(rank)" class="mw-redirect" title="Wing Commander (rank)">wing commander</a> and, for a fast-jet squadron, have an establishment of around 100 personnel and 12 aircraft.</p>
<p>The term <a href="/wiki/Squadron_(aviation)" title="Squadron (aviation)">squadron</a> can be used to refer to a sub-unit of an administrative wing or small RAF station, e.g., Air Traffic Control Squadron, Personnel Management Squadron etc. There are also Ground Support Squadrons, e.g., No 2 (Mechanical Transport) Squadron which is located at RAF Wittering. Administrative squadrons are normally commanded by a <a href="/wiki/Squadron_leader" title="Squadron leader">squadron leader</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Training_Schools">Training Schools</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Air_Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Training Schools">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Royal_Air_Force_schools" title="List of Royal Air Force schools">List of Royal Air Force schools</a></div>
<p>The RAF Schools consist of the squadrons and support apparatus that train new aircrew to join front-line squadrons. The schools separate individual streams, but group together units with similar responsibility or that operate the same aircraft type. Some schools operate with only one Squadron, and have an overall training throughput which is relatively small; some, like <a href="/wiki/No._3_Flying_Training_School_RAF" title="No. 3 Flying Training School RAF">3 FTS</a>, have responsibility for all Elementary Flying Training (EFT) in the RAF, and all RAF aircrew will pass through its squadrons when they start their flying careers. <a href="/wiki/No._2_Flying_Training_School_RAF" title="No. 2 Flying Training School RAF">2 FTS</a> and <a href="/wiki/No._6_Flying_Training_School_RAF" title="No. 6 Flying Training School RAF">6 FTS</a> do not have a front-line training responsibility - their job is to group the <a href="/wiki/University_Air_Squadron" title="University Air Squadron">University Air Squadrons</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Volunteer_Gliding_Squadron" title="Volunteer Gliding Squadron">Volunteer Gliding Squadrons</a> together. 2 FTS's commanding officer holds the only full-time flying appointment for a Group Captain in the RAF, although he is a reservist.</p>
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Central_Flying_School" title="Central Flying School">Central Flying School</a></b> – CFS standardises flying training across the air force and ensures standards and safety are maintained.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/No._1_Flying_Training_School_RAF" title="No. 1 Flying Training School RAF">No. 1 Flying Training School</a></b> - Basic Fast Jet Training (BFJT) at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Linton-on-Ouse" title="RAF Linton-on-Ouse">RAF Linton-on-Ouse</a> using <a href="/wiki/No._72_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 72 Squadron RAF">72(R) Squadron</a>'s Tucano T.1 aircraft.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/No._2_Flying_Training_School_RAF" title="No. 2 Flying Training School RAF">No. 2 Flying Training School</a> - <a href="/wiki/Volunteer_Gliding_Squadron" title="Volunteer Gliding Squadron">Volunteer Gliding Squadrons</a> &amp; the Central Gliding School, based at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Syerston" title="RAF Syerston">RAF Syerston</a></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/No._3_Flying_Training_School_RAF" title="No. 3 Flying Training School RAF">No. 3 Flying Training School</a></b> - Elementary Flying Training at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Cranwell" title="RAF Cranwell">RAF Cranwell</a> and <a href="/wiki/RAF_Wittering" title="RAF Wittering">RAF Wittering</a> with <a href="/wiki/No._57_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 57 Squadron RAF">57(R)</a> and <a href="/wiki/No._16_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 16 Squadron RAF">16(R)</a> Squadrons on the Tutor T1, as well as Multi-Engine Lead In training on <a href="/wiki/No._45_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 45 Squadron RAF">45(R) Squadron</a> on the King Air as well as training of all non-pilot aircrew.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/No._4_Flying_Training_School_RAF" title="No. 4 Flying Training School RAF">No. 4 Flying Training School</a></b> - Advanced Fast Jet Training (AFJT) at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Valley" title="RAF Valley">RAF Valley</a>, on the Hawk T.1 with <a href="/wiki/No._208_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 208 Squadron RAF">208(R) Squadron</a> &amp; Hawk T.2 aircraft of <a href="/wiki/No._4_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 4 Squadron RAF">4(R) Squadron</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/No._6_Flying_Training_School_RAF" title="No. 6 Flying Training School RAF">No. 6 Flying Training School</a> - Responsible for <a href="/wiki/No._115_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 115 Squadron RAF">115(R) Squadron</a> and the <a href="/wiki/University_Air_Squadron" title="University Air Squadron">University Air Squadrons</a> which fly the Tutor T1<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>The British military operate a number of joint training organisations based at RAF Stations:</p>
<ul>
<li><b>Defence Elementary Flying Training School</b> - EFT for Royal Navy and Army Air Corps students at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Barkston_Heath" title="RAF Barkston Heath">RAF Barkston Heath</a>, comprising <a href="/wiki/703_Naval_Air_Squadron" title="703 Naval Air Squadron">703 Naval Air Squadron</a> and <a href="/wiki/No._674_Squadron_AAC" title="No. 674 Squadron AAC">No. 674 Squadron AAC</a></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Defence_Helicopter_Flying_School" title="Defence Helicopter Flying School">Defence Helicopter Flying School</a></b> - A Tri-service school combining <a href="/wiki/705_Naval_Air_Squadron" title="705 Naval Air Squadron">705 Naval Air Squadron</a> and <a href="/wiki/No._660_Squadron_AAC" title="No. 660 Squadron AAC">No. 660 Squadron AAC</a> on the Squirrel HT.1 for basic helicopter training, which take alternate intakes from all three services, and the RAF's <a href="/wiki/No._60_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 60 Squadron RAF">60(R) Squadron</a> for advanced flying training on the Griffin HT.1. Based at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Shawbury" title="RAF Shawbury">RAF Shawbury</a>.</li>
<li><b>Defence College of Technical Training</b><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> - Part of <a href="/wiki/No._22_Group_RAF" title="No. 22 Group RAF">22(Training) Group</a> it educates personnel from all three Services in several different specialist areas: aeronautical engineering, electro and mechanical engineering, marine engineering, and communication and information systems.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup>
<ul>
<li>Defence College of Aeronautical Engineering.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> Comprising:
<ul>
<li>Royal Naval Air Engineering and Survival Equipment School.</li>
<li>DCAE (Lyneham)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/No._1_School_of_Technical_Training_RAF" title="No. 1 School of Technical Training RAF">No 1 School of Technical Training</a>, Royal Air Force</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Defence_College_of_Electro-Mechanical_Engineering" title="Defence College of Electro-Mechanical Engineering">Defence College of Electro-Mechanical Engineering</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> Comprising:
<ul>
<li>Royal Naval School of Marine Engineering</li>
<li>8 Battalion Royal Electrical and Mechanical Engineers</li>
<li>No 4 School of Technical Training, Royal Air Force</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Defence_College_of_Communications_and_Information_Systems" title="Defence College of Communications and Information Systems">Defence School of Communications and Information Systems</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> Comprising:
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Royal_School_of_Signals" title="Royal School of Signals">11th (Royal School of Signals) Signal Regiment</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/No._1_Radio_School_RAF" title="No. 1 Radio School RAF">No 1 Radio School</a>, Royal Air Force</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Defence School of Marine Engineering.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flights">Flights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Air_Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Flights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mount_Pleasant_Airport_-_Donald_Morrison.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Mount_Pleasant_Airport_-_Donald_Morrison.jpg/220px-Mount_Pleasant_Airport_-_Donald_Morrison.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Mount_Pleasant_Airport_-_Donald_Morrison.jpg/330px-Mount_Pleasant_Airport_-_Donald_Morrison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Mount_Pleasant_Airport_-_Donald_Morrison.jpg/440px-Mount_Pleasant_Airport_-_Donald_Morrison.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3888" data-file-height="2592" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mount_Pleasant_Airport_-_Donald_Morrison.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/RAF_Mount_Pleasant" title="RAF Mount Pleasant">RAF Mount Pleasant</a>, home to <a href="/wiki/No._1435_Flight_RAF" title="No. 1435 Flight RAF">No. 1435 Flight</a> providing air defence for the <a href="/wiki/Falkland_Islands" title="Falkland Islands">Falkland Islands</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Royal_Air_Force_aircraft_independent_flights" title="List of Royal Air Force aircraft independent flights">List of Royal Air Force aircraft independent flights</a></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Flight_(military_unit)" title="Flight (military unit)">flight</a> is a sub-division of a squadron. Flying squadrons are often divided into two flights, e.g., "A" and "B", each under the command of a <a href="/wiki/Squadron_leader" title="Squadron leader">squadron leader</a>. Administrative squadrons on a station are also divided into flights and these flights are commanded by a <a href="/wiki/Junior_officer" title="Junior officer">junior officer</a>, often a <a href="/wiki/Flight_lieutenant" title="Flight lieutenant">flight lieutenant</a>. Because of their small size, there are several flying units formed as flights rather than squadrons. For example, <a href="/wiki/No._1435_Flight_RAF" title="No. 1435 Flight RAF">No. 1435 Flight</a> is based at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Mount_Pleasant" title="RAF Mount Pleasant">RAF Mount Pleasant</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Falkland_Islands" title="Falkland Islands">Falkland Islands</a>, maintaining air defence cover with four Eurofighter Typhoon aircraft.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personnel">Personnel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Air_Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Personnel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Royal_Air_Force_members" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Royal Air Force members">List of Royal Air Force members</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_comparative_military_ranks" title="List of comparative military ranks">List of comparative military ranks</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RAF_Regiment_(8658943968)_(2).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/RAF_Regiment_%288658943968%29_%282%29.jpg/220px-RAF_Regiment_%288658943968%29_%282%29.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/RAF_Regiment_%288658943968%29_%282%29.jpg/330px-RAF_Regiment_%288658943968%29_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/RAF_Regiment_%288658943968%29_%282%29.jpg/440px-RAF_Regiment_%288658943968%29_%282%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4925" data-file-height="3284" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RAF_Regiment_(8658943968)_(2).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Members of the <a href="/wiki/RAF_Regiment" title="RAF Regiment">RAF Regiment</a> on parade, 2013</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At its height in 1944 during the Second World War, more than 1,100,000 personnel were serving in the RAF. The longest-lived founding member of the RAF was <a href="/wiki/Henry_Allingham" title="Henry Allingham">Henry Allingham</a>, who died on 18 July 2009 aged 113.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of 1 January 2015, the Royal Air Force numbered some 34,200 Regular<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> and 1,940 <a href="/wiki/Royal_Auxiliary_Air_Force" title="Royal Auxiliary Air Force">Royal Auxiliary Air Force</a><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> personnel, giving a combined component strength of 36,140 personnel. In addition to the active elements of the Royal Air Force, (Regular and Royal Auxiliary Air Force), all ex-Regular personnel remain liable to be recalled for duty in <a href="/wiki/Total_war" title="Total war">a time of need</a>, this is known as the <a href="/wiki/Regular_Reserve_(United_Kingdom)" title="Regular Reserve (United Kingdom)">Regular Reserve</a>. In 2007 there were 33,980 Regular Reserves of the Royal Air Force, of which 7,950 served under a fixed-term reserve contract.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> Publications since April 2013 no-longer report the entire strength of the Regular Reserve, instead they only give a figure for Regular Reserves who serve under a fixed-term reserve contract.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup> They had a strength of 7,120 personnel in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-DASA-Reserves_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DASA-Reserves-44">[43]</a></sup> All personnel figures exclude the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force_Volunteer_Reserve" title="Royal Air Force Volunteer Reserve">Royal Air Force Volunteer Reserve</a> and its associated <a href="/wiki/University_Air_Squadron" title="University Air Squadron">University Air Squadrons</a>.</p>
<p>The Royal Air Force Volunteer Reserve is another reserve element of the RAF, however unlike the ones described above, they have no call-up liability. Instead, they are responsible for the management and operation of the <a href="/wiki/Air_Training_Corps" title="Air Training Corps">Air Training Corps</a>, Combined Cadet Force RAF Sections (CCF(RAF)), <a href="/wiki/Volunteer_Gliding_Squadron" title="Volunteer Gliding Squadron">Volunteer Gliding Squadrons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Air_Experience_Flights" class="mw-redirect" title="Air Experience Flights">Air Experience Flights</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_Air_Squadron" title="University Air Squadron">University Air Squadron</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Defence_Technical_Undergraduate_Scheme" title="Defence Technical Undergraduate Scheme">Defence Technical Undergraduate Scheme</a>.</p>
<p>Figures provided by the <a href="/wiki/International_Institute_for_Strategic_Studies" title="International Institute for Strategic Studies">International Institute for Strategic Studies</a> from 2012 showed that Royal Air Force pilots achieve a relatively high number of flying hours per year when compared with other major <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> allies such as France and Germany. RAF pilots achieve 210 to 290 flying hours per year.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup> French and <a href="/wiki/German_Air_Force" title="German Air Force">German Air Force</a> pilots achieved only 180 and 150 flying hours across their fleets respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Officers">Officers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Air_Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Officers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/RAF_officer_ranks" title="RAF officer ranks">RAF officer ranks</a></div>
<p>Officers hold a <a href="/wiki/Letters_patent" title="Letters patent">commission</a> from the <a href="/wiki/British_monarchy" class="mw-redirect" title="British monarchy">Sovereign</a>, which provides the legal authority for them to issue orders to subordinates. The commission of a regular officer is granted after successfully completing the 24-week-long Initial Officer Training<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> course at the <a href="/wiki/RAF_College_Cranwell" class="mw-redirect" title="RAF College Cranwell">RAF College, Cranwell</a>, Lincolnshire. Other officers also train at RAF Cranwell, but on different courses, such as those for professionally qualified officers.</p>
<p>The titles and insignia of RAF officers were chiefly derived from those used by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a>, specifically the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Naval_Air_Service" title="Royal Naval Air Service">Royal Naval Air Service</a> (RNAS) during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>. For example, the rank of <a href="/wiki/Squadron_Leader" class="mw-redirect" title="Squadron Leader">Squadron Leader</a> derived its name from the RNAS rank of Squadron Commander. RAF officers fall into three categories: <a href="/wiki/Air_Officer" class="mw-redirect" title="Air Officer">air officers</a>, senior officers and <a href="/wiki/Junior_officer" title="Junior officer">junior officers</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_ranks">Other ranks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Air_Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Other ranks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/RAF_other_ranks" title="RAF other ranks">RAF other ranks</a></div>
<p>Other ranks attend the Recruit Training Squadron at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Halton" title="RAF Halton">RAF Halton</a> for basic training.</p>
<p>The titles and insignia of other ranks in the RAF were based on that of the Army, with some alterations in terminology. Over the years, this structure has seen significant changes: for example, there was once a separate system for those in technical trades, and the ranks of <a href="/wiki/Chief_Technician" class="mw-redirect" title="Chief Technician">Chief Technician</a> and <a href="/wiki/Junior_Technician" class="mw-redirect" title="Junior Technician">Junior Technician</a> continue to be held only by personnel in technical trades. RAF other ranks fall into four categories: Warrant Officers, Senior Non-Commissioned Officers, Junior Non-Commissioned Officers and Airmen.</p>
<p>All Warrant Officers in the RAF are equal in terms of rank, but the most senior Non-Commissioned appointment is known as the <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_the_Air_Staff%27s_Warrant_Officer" title="Chief of the Air Staff's Warrant Officer">Chief of the Air Staff's Warrant Officer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Chief_of_the_Air_Staff.27s_Warrant_Officer_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chief_of_the_Air_Staff.27s_Warrant_Officer-48">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Branches_and_trades">Branches and trades</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_Air_Force&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Branches and trades">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tornado_navigator_DN-SD-01-05054.JPEG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Tornado_navigator_DN-SD-01-05054.JPEG/170px-Tornado_navigator_DN-SD-01-05054.JPEG" width="170" height="211" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Tornado_navigator_DN-SD-01-05054.JPEG/255px-Tornado_navigator_DN-SD-01-05054.JPEG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Tornado_navigator_DN-SD-01-05054.JPEG/340px-Tornado_navigator_DN-SD-01-05054.JPEG 2x" data-file-width="901" data-file-height="1117" /></a>
